\subsection{Classification: Groups of Special
Orders}\label{classification-groups-of-special-orders}}

General strategy: find a normal subgroup (usually a Sylow) and use
recognition of semidirect products.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \href{https://kconrad.math.uconn.edu/blurbs/grouptheory/group12.pdf}{Keith
  Conrad: Classifying Groups of Order 12}
\item
  Order \(pqr\): ?
\item
  Order \(p^2q\): ?
\end{itemize}

\begin{proposition}[Classification of groups of order $p$]

Every group \(G\) of prime order \(p\geq 2\) is cyclic and thus
isomorphic to \({\mathbb{Z}}/p\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[?]

Supposing that \(g\neq e\), it generates a cyclic subgroup
\(H \coloneqq\left\langle{g}\right\rangle \leq G\) of order dividing
\(p\) by Lagrange. Since \(g\neq e\), \(\#H = p = \# G\).

\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Classification of groups of order $p^2$]

Every group \(G\) of order \(p^2\) is abelian, and thus isomorphic to
either \(C_{p^2}\) or \(C_p^2\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[?]

Quotient by the center to get
\(m\coloneqq\#G/Z(G) \in \left\{{ 1, p, p^2 }\right\}\). By cases:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Since \(G\) is a \(p{\hbox{-}}\)group, \(G\) has nontrivial center, so
  \(m\neq 1\)
\item
  If \(m=p\), then \(G/Z(G)\) is cyclic and thus \(G\) is abelian by the
  \(G/Z(G)\) theorem.
\item
  If \(m=p^2\), \(Z(G) = G\) and \(G\) is abelian, done.
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Classification of groups of order $pq$]

If \(G\) is a group of order \(pq\) where without loss of generality
\(q<p\), then

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(q\notdivides p-1\) then \(G\) is cyclic and
  \(G\cong S_p \times S_q \cong C_{pq}\).
\item
  If \(q\divides p-1\) then \(G\cong S_q \rtimes_\psi S_p\) where
  \(S_p {~\trianglelefteq~}G\) and
  \(\psi: S_q \to \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(S_p)\), and \(G\) has a
  presentation
  \begin{align*}
  G\cong \left\langle{a, b {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}a^p, b^q, bab^{-1}= a^\ell}\right\rangle \\ \\ 
  \ell \not\equiv 1 \operatorname{mod}p && \ell^q \equiv 1 \operatorname{mod}p
  .\end{align*}
\end{enumerate}

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[of $pq$ theorem, case 1]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Suppose \(q<p\).
\item
  Apply the Sylow theorems to \(p\):

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(n_p \cong 1 \operatorname{mod}p \implies n_p \in \left\{{ 1, p+1, 2p+1, \cdots }\right\}\).
  \item
    \(n_p \divides q \implies n_p \in \left\{{ 1, q }\right\}\).
  \item
    Since \(1<q<p<p+1\), this forces \(n_p = 1\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Suppose \(q\notdivides p-1\) and apply the Sylow theorems to \(q\):

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(n_q \equiv 1\operatorname{mod}q \implies n_q \in \left\{{ 1, q+1, 2q+1,\cdots }\right\}\)
  \item
    \(n_q \divides p \implies n_q\in \left\{{ 1, p }\right\}\)
  \item
    Now note that if \(n_q\neq 1\), then \(n_q=p\) and \(p\) is of the
    form \(kq+1\) for some \(k\).
  \item
    \textbf{Use of assumption}: then
    \(p=kq+1 \iff p-1 = kq \iff q\divides p-1\), which is precisely what
    we assumed is \emph{not} the case.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  So \(n_p = n_q = 1\) and \(S_p, S_q {~\trianglelefteq~}G\).
\item
  Apply recognition of direct products:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(S_p, S_q \leq G\): check.
  \item
    \(S_p, S_q {~\trianglelefteq~}G\): check.
  \item
    \(S_p \cap S_q = \left\{{ e }\right\}\): check, because they are
    coprime order.
  \item
    \(S_p S_q = G\): follows from a counting argument:
    \begin{align*}
    \# S_pS_q = {\# S_p \# S_q \over \# \qty{S_p \cap S_q}} = {pq \over 1} = \# G
    .\end{align*}
    If \(G\) is finite, then \(AB\leq G\) with \(\#AB = \#G\) implies
    \(AB = G\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[of $pq$ theorem, case 2]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Suppose \(q \divides p-1\), the previous argument for \(S_p\) works,
  but the argument for \(S_q\) doesn't, so we get a semidirect product.
\item
  Work up to isomorphism:
  \begin{align*}
  S_p \cong {\mathbb{Z}}/p = \left\langle{a{~\mathrel{\Big|}~}a^p}\right\rangle
  &{~\trianglelefteq~}G \\ \\
  S_q \cong {\mathbb{Z}}/q =\left\langle{b{~\mathrel{\Big|}~}b^q}\right\rangle
  &\leq G
  .\end{align*}
\item
  We have
  \begin{align*}
  G&\cong {\mathbb{Z}}/q \rtimes_{\psi} {\mathbb{Z}}/p
  && \psi: {\mathbb{Z}}/q \to \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}\qty{{\mathbb{Z}}/p} \\ \\
  \implies G &\cong\left\langle{a,b {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}a^p, b^q,\,\, aba^{-1}= \psi(b) = b^\ell }\right\rangle && \text{for some }\ell
  .\end{align*}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Since \({\mathbb{Z}}/q\) is cyclic, such a morphism is determined by
    the image of the generator \([1]_q \in {\mathbb{Z}}/q\).
  \item
    Note that \([1]_q \mapsto \operatorname{id}_{{\mathbb{Z}}/p}\) is
    such a morphism, and yields the direct product again.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Identify
  \(\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}({\mathbb{Z}}/p) \cong \qty{\qty{{\mathbb{Z}}/p}^{\times}, \times } \cong \qty{ {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1), +}\).
\item
  So we need to classify morphisms
  \begin{align*}
  \psi: {\mathbb{Z}}/q\to {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)
  .\end{align*}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Consider \(\operatorname{im}\psi \leq {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)\).
  \item
    Sending \([1]_q\) to the identity in
    \(\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}({\mathbb{Z}}/p)\) yields the direct product
    again, so pick nontrivial morphisms.
  \item
    Since \(\# \operatorname{im}\psi \divides q\) which is prime, its
    order is equal to \(q\).
  \item
    Since \(q\divides p-1\) and \({\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)\) is cyclic of
    order \(p-1\), by Cauchy's theorem there is a unique subgroup of
    order \(q\), say \(C_q \leq {\mathbb{Z}}(p-1)\)
  \item
    We can send \([1]_q\) to \([\alpha]_{p-1} \in {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)\)
    where \(\alpha\) is any generator of \(C_q\), of which there are
    \(\phi(q) = q-1\) nontrivial choices.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Thus there are \(q-1\) distinct nontrivial choices for the action
  \(\psi: {\mathbb{Z}}/q \to {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)\).
\end{itemize}

\begin{claim}

All choices yield isomorphic semidirect products.

\end{claim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Use that \(G\coloneqq A\rtimes_{\psi} N\) with
  \(\psi:A\to \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(N)\) is an
  \(\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(N)\) and \(\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(A)\) module,
  where \(f \in \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(N)\) and
  \(\pi\in \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(A)\) act in the following ways:
  \begin{align*}
  \pi &\curvearrowright A\rtimes_\psi N = A \rtimes_{\psi \circ \pi } N \\
  f &\curvearrowright A\rtimes_\psi N = A \rtimes_{\gamma_f \circ \psi } N
  .\end{align*}
  where
  \begin{align*}
  \gamma_f: \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(N) &\to \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(N) \\
  \psi &\mapsto f\circ \psi \circ f^{-1}
  .\end{align*}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    These actions preserve the group isomorphism type of \(G\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  However, since \(C_q \leq {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)\) and
  \(\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(C_q) \cong {\mathbb{Z}}/(q-1)\), there are
  exactly \(q-1\) automorphisms of the image \(C_q\), say
  \(\left\{{\pi_k}\right\}_{k=1}^{q-1}\).
\item
  So \(\psi \circ \pi_k: {\mathbb{Z}}/q \to {\mathbb{Z}}/(p-1)\) for
  \(1\leq k \leq q-1\) yields \(q-1\) distinct actions, and we're done.
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Frattini's Argument]

If \(N{~\trianglelefteq~}G\) and \(P\in {\operatorname{Syl}}_p(H)\) then
\(G = N_G(P)H\).

\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}[?]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Let \(g\in G\), then since \(P\leq H {~\trianglelefteq~}G\) we have
  \(gPg^{-1}\subseteq gHg^{-1}= H\).
\item
  So \(P' \coloneqq gPg^{-1}\in {\operatorname{Syl}}_p(H)\) for all
  \(g\), and since Sylows in \(H\) are all conjugate, we can write
  \(P' = h^{-1}Ph^{-1}\) for some \(h\in H\).
\item
  This says \(hPh^{-1}= gPg^{-1}\) and thus
  \(P = (g^{-1}h)P (h^{-1}g) = (h^{-1}g)^{-1}P (h^{-1}g)\).
\item
  But then \(g^{-1}h \in N_G(P)\) so \(g\in N_G(P)H\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[$p$ groups are solvable]

Every finite \(p\) group is solvable.

\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}[?]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  By induction on \(k\) in \(\# G = p^k\): if \(\# G = p\) then \(G\) is
  abelian and automatically solvable.
\item
  Inductively, for \(\# G = p^k\), now consider \(Z(G)\neq 1\) since
  we're in a \(p{\hbox{-}}\)group.
\item
  If \(G/Z(G)\) is abelian, use the general fact: \(H\) solvable and
  \(G/H\) solvable implies \(G\) solvable.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Here \(Z(G)\) and \(G/Z(G)\) are both abelian and thus solvable.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Otherwise \(G/Z(G)\) is a \(p{\hbox{-}}\)group of size \(p^{k-1}\) and
  thus solvable by hypothesis.
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[$pq$ groups have normals the size of the biggest prime]

If \(\# G = pq\) with \(p<q\) distinct primes, then \(G\) has a normal
subgroup of size \(q\).

This is immediate from Sylow theory:
\([n_q]_q = 1, n_q \divides p, p<q\) forces \(n_q = 1\).

\end{lemma}

\begin{proposition}[PQR Theorem]

If \(|G| = pqr\) where \(p<q<r\) are distinct primes then \(G\) is
solvable.

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[?]

\envlist

Idea:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Get a normal subgroup \(R\) of order \(r\), so \(\# (G/R) = pq\).
\item
  Get a normal subgroup \(Q_1\) of order \(q\) in \(G/R\), which
  corresponds to \(Q{~\trianglelefteq~}G\) of order \(qr\) containing
  \(R\). Note that \(R{~\trianglelefteq~}Q\) since normality descends to
  subgroups.
\item
  Now \(G\to Q\to R \to 1\) is a subnormal series whose quotients are
  all cyclic and thus abelian:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(\#(G/Q) = pqr/qr = p\),
  \item
    \(\#(Q/R) = qr/r = q\),
  \item
    \(\#(R/1) = r\),
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{remark}

Proof of first claim: let \(m\coloneqq\# G = pqr\), then \(G\) has a
normal subgroup of order \(r\).

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Claim: at least one of the Sylows for \(p,q,\) or \(r\) is normal.

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    If none of the Sylow \(p,q,r\) groups are normal, then
    \(n_r \geq r\) and \(n_p \geq q\). Counting the contributions from
    just \({\operatorname{Syl}}_q(G)\) and \({\operatorname{Syl}}_p(G)\)
    yields
    \begin{align*}
    n_q(q-1) + n_r(r-1) \geq pr(q-1) + pq(r-1) = pqr + p(qr -q - r)
    .\end{align*}
  \item
    If this is to be at most \(m\), it must be that \(qr-q-r\) is
    negative (since \(p>1\) and otherwise this would yield more than
    \(pqr\) elements).
  \item
    But if this holds,
    \begin{align*}
    qr-q-r \leq 0 \iff q(r-1) \leq r \iff q\leq {r\over r-1}
    .\end{align*}
    But \(q>2\) be assumption, and \(1\leq {r\over r-1}\leq 2\) for any
    number \(r\). \(\contradiction\).
  \item
    So there is one of \(S_p , S_q, S_r\) that is normal in \(G\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Now if \(S_r\) is normal we're done, so suppose not and \(n_r > 1\).
  Claim: we can get another subgroup of order \(r\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Let \(N\) be the normal Sylow, so either
    \(N\in {\operatorname{Syl}}_p(G)\) or
    \(N\in {\operatorname{Syl}}_q(G)\).
  \item
    Then \(G/N\) has order \(r\ell\) for either \(\ell = q\) or
    \(\ell = p\) respectively.
  \item
    In either case, \(\ell < r\). Using the lemma, \(G/N\) has a normal
    subgroup of size \(r\), say \(R/N \leq G/N\).
  \item
    Then by the subgroup correspondence theorem, \(R\) corresponds to a
    normal subgroup \(R'{~\trianglelefteq~}G\) of size \(r\ell\) with
    \(r<\ell\).
  \item
    Applying the same lemma to \(R'\) immediately yields a normal
    subgroup \(R''\) of order \(r\) in \(R'\)
  \item
    Now use that \(R'' \operatorname{char} R'\) since Sylows are
    characteristic, and \(R'{~\trianglelefteq~}G\), so
    \(R''{~\trianglelefteq~}G\) too.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{remark}

\hypertarget{series-of-groups}{%
\subsection{Series of Groups}\label{series-of-groups}}

\begin{definition}[Normal Series]

A \textbf{normal series} of a group \(G\) is a sequence
\(G \to G^1 \to G^2 \to \cdots\) such that
\(G^{i+1} {~\trianglelefteq~}G_i\) for every \(i\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Central Series]

A \textbf{central series} for a group \(G\) is a terminating normal
series \(G \to G^1 \to \cdots \to \left\{{e}\right\}\) such that each
quotient is \textbf{central}, i.e.~\([G, G^i] \leq G^{i-1}\) for all
\(i\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Composition Series]

A \textbf{composition series} of a group \(G\) is a finite normal series
such that \(G^{i+1}\) is a \emph{maximal proper} normal subgroup of
\(G^i\).

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Jordan-Holder]

Any two composition series of a group have the same length and
isomorphic composition factors (up to permutation).

\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}[Simple Groups]

A group \(G\) is \textbf{simple} iff
\(H{~\trianglelefteq~}G \implies H = \left\{{e}\right\}, G\), i.e.~it
has no non-trivial proper subgroups.

\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}

If \(G\) is \emph{not} simple, then \(G\) is an extension of any of its
normal subgroups. I.e. for any \(N{~\trianglelefteq~}G\), \(G \cong E\)
for some extension of the form \(N\to E\to G/N\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{definition}[Lower Central Series]

Set \(G^0 = G\) and \(G^{i+1} = [G, G^i]\), then
\(G^0 \geq G^1 \geq \cdots\) is the \emph{lower central series} of
\(G\).

\end{definition}

\begin{quote}
Mnemonic: ``lower'' because the chain is descending. Iterate the adjoint
map \([{-}, G]\), if this terminates then the map is nilpotent, so call
\(G\) nilpotent!
\end{quote}

\begin{definition}[Upper Central Series]

Set \(Z_0 = 1\), \(Z_1 = Z(G)\), and \(Z_{i+1} \leq G\) to be the
subgroup satisfying \(Z_{i+1}/Z_i = Z(G/Z_i)\). Then
\(Z_0 \leq Z_1 \leq \cdots\) is the \emph{upper central series} of
\(G\).

Equivalently, since \(Z_i{~\trianglelefteq~}G\), there is a quotient map
\(\pi:G\to G/Z_i\), so define \(Z_{i+1} \coloneqq\pi^{-1}(Z(G/Z_i))\)
(?).

\begin{quote}
Mnemonic: ``upper'' because the chain is ascending. ``Take higher
centers''.
\end{quote}

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Derived Series]

Set \(G^{(0)} = G\) and \(G^{(i+1)} = [G^{(i)}, G^{(i)}]\), then
\(G^{(0)} \geq G^{(1)} \geq \cdots\) is the \emph{derived series} of
\(G\).

\end{definition}

\hypertarget{solvability}{%
\subsection{Solvability}\label{solvability}}

\begin{remark}

A useful way to extract normal subgroups: let \(G\) act on literally
anything by \(\phi: G\to \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(X)\). Then
\(\ker \phi {~\trianglelefteq~}G\) is always a normal subgroup. Some
examples:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(G\curvearrowright G\) by \(x\mapsto gx\).
\item
  \(G\curvearrowright\left\{{H\leq G}\right\}\) by \(H\mapsto gH\) or
  \(H\mapsto gHg^{-1}\).
\item
  \(G\curvearrowright\left\{{{\operatorname{Syl}}_p(G)}\right\}\) for a
  fixed \(p\) by \(S_p \mapsto gS_p g^{-1}\).
\item
  \(G\curvearrowright H\) for \(H{~\trianglelefteq~}G\) by inner
  automorphisms \(h\mapsto ghg^{-1}\).
\end{itemize}

\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Solvable]

A group \(G\) is \textbf{solvable} iff \(G\) has a terminating normal
series with abelian composition factors, i.e.
\begin{align*}
G \coloneqq G_n > G_{n-1} > \cdots > G_2 > G_1 \coloneqq\left\{{ e }\right\} && \text{ with } G^{i}/G^{i+1}\text{ abelian for all } i
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{remark}

If \(G = { \mathsf{Gal}} (L/K)\) is a Galois group corresponding to a
polynomial \(f\), then \(G\) is solvable as a group iff \(f\) is
solvable in radicals: there is a tower of extensions
\(K = F_0 \subset F_1 \subset F_2 \subset \cdots \subset F_m = L\) where

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(F_i = F_{i-1}(\alpha_i)\) where \(\alpha_i^{m_i } \in F_{i-1}\) for
  some power \(m_i \in {\mathbb{Z}}^{\geq 0}\), and
\item
  \(F_m \supseteq \operatorname{SF}(f)\) contains a splitting field for
  \(f\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Characterization of Solvable]

A group \(G\) is solvable iff its derived series terminates.

\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[$S_n$ is Almost Always Solvable]

If \(n\geq 4\) then \(S_n\) is solvable.

\end{theorem}

\begin{fact}

Some useful facts about solvable groups:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(G\) is solvable iff \(G\) has a terminating \emph{derived series}.
\item
  Solvable groups satisfy the 2 out of 3 property
\item
  Abelian \(\implies\) solvable
\item
  Every group of order less than 60 is solvable.
\end{itemize}

\end{fact}

\hypertarget{ring-theory}{%
\section{Ring Theory}\label{ring-theory}}

\begin{proposition}[Subring criteria]

A subset \(S\subseteq R\) is a subring iff

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \((S, +)\) forms an abelian subgroup (so closed under addition and
  contains inverses)
\item
  \((S, \cdot)\) forms a submonoid (so closed under multiplication)
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Ideal Operations]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(I + J = \left\{{i+j {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}i\in I, j\in J}\right\} = \left\langle{I, J}\right\rangle\)
  is the smallest ideal containing \(I\) and \(J\).
\item
  \(IJ = \left\{{\sum_{k\leq N} x_k y_k {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}x_k\in I, y_k \in J, N\in {\mathbb{Z}}^{\geq 0}}\right\}\)
  is the ideal generated by all finite sums of products.
\item
  \(I \cap J\) is an ideal, \(I\cup J\) is generally \textbf{not} an
  ideal
\item
  Ideals are \emph{comaximal} if
  \(I + J = \left\langle{ 1 }\right\rangle\).
\item
  If \(I+J = \left\langle{ 1 }\right\rangle\) then \(I \cap J = IJ\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{definition}[Ideal generated by a set]

The ideal \textbf{generated} by \(\left\{{a, b}\right\}\) is defined as
\begin{align*}
\left\langle{a, b}\right\rangle \coloneqq Ra + Rb \coloneqq\left\{{ r_1 a + r_2 b {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}r_i \in R}\right\}
.\end{align*}

More generally for a set \(S = \left\{{s_k}\right\}\),
\begin{align*}
\left\langle{S}\right\rangle \coloneqq\sum_{k=1}^{\# S} Rs_k \coloneqq\left\{{ \sum r_k s_k {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}r_k\in R, s_k\in S}\right\}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[?]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\left\langle{p, q}\right\rangle = \left\langle{\gcd(p, q)}\right\rangle {~\trianglelefteq~}{\mathbb{Z}}\).
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\hypertarget{isomorphism-theorems-1}{%
\subsection{Isomorphism Theorems}\label{isomorphism-theorems-1}}

\begin{remark}

These are all basically the same for modules.

\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[First Isomorphism Theorem]

For any ring morphism \(f:A\to B\) there is SES of rings
\begin{align*}
0 \to \ker f \to A \to \operatorname{im}(f) \to 0
,\end{align*}
and thus \(A/\ker f \cong \operatorname{im}f\). If \(f\) is surjective,
then \(A/\ker f \cong B\).

More traditionally stated:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\ker \phi \in \operatorname{Id}(A)\)
\item
  \(\operatorname{im}\phi \leq B\) is a subring (not necessarily an
  ideal)
\item
  \(R/\ker \phi \cong \operatorname{im}\phi\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Second Isomorphism Theorem]

Let \(R\in \mathsf{Ring}, S\leq R, I\in \operatorname{Id}(R)\), then
there is an isomorphism:
\begin{align*}
{S+I \over I} {\xrightarrow{\sim}}{S\over S \cap I}
.\end{align*}

Where it's also true that this statement makes sense:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(S+I \leq R\) is a subring.
\item
  \(S \cap I {~\trianglelefteq~}S\)
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Third Isomorphism Theorem]

For \(I\in \operatorname{Id}(R)\), the canonical quotient map
\(\phi: R \to R/I\) induces a bijective correspondence:
\begin{align*}
\left\{{\substack{
  J \in \operatorname{Id}(R) {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}J\supseteq I
}}\right\}
&\rightleftharpoons
  \operatorname{Id}(R/I) \\
J \coloneqq\phi^{-1}(\mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muJ\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu) &\mapsfrom \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muJ\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu \\
J &\mapsto \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muJ\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu \coloneqq\phi(J) 
,\end{align*}
where \(\phi: R\to R/I\) is the canonical quotient morphism.

More traditionally:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If \(S, I \in \operatorname{Id}(R)\) with \(S\) containing \(I\) then
  \begin{align*}
  S/I \leq R/I
  .\end{align*}
\item
  Every ideal in \(\operatorname{Id}(R/I)\) is of the form
  \(\mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muS\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu \coloneqq S/I\)
  for some \(S\in \operatorname{Id}(R)\) containing \(I\).
\item
  If \(I, J \in \operatorname{Id}(R)\) with
  \(I \subseteq J \subseteq R\) then there is an isomorphism
  \begin{align*}
  {R/I \over J/I} {\xrightarrow{\sim}}{R\over J}
  .\end{align*}
\end{itemize}

Moreover, \(A\leq R\) is a subring containing \(I\) iff
\(A/I \in \operatorname{Id}(R/I)\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that if \(J\in \operatorname{Id}(R)\) (with \(J\supseteq I\)) is
radical/prime/maximal iff
\(\mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muJ\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu \in \operatorname{Id}(R/I)\)
is radical/prime/maximal.

\end{exercise}

\hypertarget{important-techniques}{%
\subsection{Important Techniques}\label{important-techniques}}

\begin{proposition}[Fields are simple]

\(R \in \mathsf{Field}\iff \operatorname{Id}(R) = \left\{{ 0, R }\right\}\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[?]

\(\implies\): If \(0\neq x\in I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\), using that
\(R^{\bullet}= R^{\times}\), \(x\) is a unit. So \(x^{-1}\in R\), and
\(xx^{-1}\coloneqq 1 \in I\) so \(I = R\).

\(\impliedby\): Let \(x\in R^{\bullet}\), then \(Rx = R\) so \(1\in Rx\)
and \(1=rx\) for some \(r\in R\). This forces \(x=r^{-1}\).

\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Showing ideals are maximal/prime with quotients]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(R/{\mathfrak{m}}\) is a field
  \(\iff {\mathfrak{m}}\in \operatorname{mSpec}(R)\) is maximal.
\item
  \(R/{\mathfrak{p}}\) is an integral domain
  \(\iff {\mathfrak{p}}\in \operatorname{Spec}(R)\) is prime.
\item
  \(R/J\) is reduced \(\iff J\) is radical.
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[of 1]

Use the ideal correspondence theorem:
\(\operatorname{Id}(R/{\mathfrak{m}})\) are ideals of \(R\) containing
\({\mathfrak{m}}\):
\begin{align*}
&\quad R/{\mathfrak{m}}\in \mathsf{Field}\\
&\iff \not\exists J/{\mathfrak{m}}\in \operatorname{Id}(R/{\mathfrak{m}})^{\bullet}\text{ such that } J \in \operatorname{Id}(R) \\
&\iff \not\exists {\mathfrak{m}}\subsetneq J \subsetneq R \\
&\iff J\in \operatorname{mSpec}(R)
.\end{align*}

\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[of 2]

\(\impliedby\): Show \(xy = 0\) with \(x\neq 0\) forces \(y=0\). Let
\(x, y\in {\mathfrak{p}}\in \operatorname{Spec}R\), so
\(x = a + I, y = b + I\) for some \(a,b\in R\). If
\(xy=0 \operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}\) with
\(y\neq 0\operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}\), we can check
\begin{align*}
xy = (a+{\mathfrak{p}})(b+{\mathfrak{p}}) \coloneqq(ab) + {\mathfrak{p}}= 0 + {\mathfrak{p}}\implies ab\in {\mathfrak{p}}
.\end{align*}
Since \({\mathfrak{p}}\) is prime and
\(x\neq 0 \implies a\not\in{\mathfrak{p}}\), so \(b\in {\mathfrak{p}}\).
But then
\begin{align*}
y \coloneqq b + {\mathfrak{p}}= 0 + {\mathfrak{p}}= 0 \operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}
.\end{align*}

\(\implies\): Let \(a,b\in R\) with \(xy\in {\mathfrak{p}}\), we want to
show that if \(x\not \in {\mathfrak{p}}\) then \(y\in {\mathfrak{p}}\).
Note
\(x\not \in {\mathfrak{p}}\iff x \cong 0 \operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}\).
Setting \(x\coloneqq a + {\mathfrak{p}}, y\coloneqq b + {\mathfrak{p}}\)
yields
\begin{align*}
xy \coloneqq(a+{\mathfrak{p}})(b+{\mathfrak{p}}) \coloneqq ab + {\mathfrak{p}}= 0 \operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}
.\end{align*}
Since \(R/{\mathfrak{p}}\) is a domain, assuming
\(x\neq 0 \operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}\) we have
\(y = 0 \operatorname{mod}{\mathfrak{p}}\), so \(y\in {\mathfrak{p}}\).

\end{proof}

\begin{remark}

Note that this yields a quick proof that
\(\operatorname{mSpec}R \subseteq \operatorname{Spec}R\), using that
\(\mathsf{Field}\leq \mathsf{IntDomain}\):
\begin{align*}
I \text{ maximal } \iff R/I \in \mathsf{Field}{\color{blue} \implies } R/I \in \mathsf{IntDomain} \iff I \text{ prime}
.\end{align*}

\end{remark}

\begin{fact}

If \({\mathfrak{m}}\) is maximal and \(x \in R\setminus{\mathfrak{m}}\)
then \({\mathfrak{m}}+ Rx = R = \left\langle{ 1}\right\rangle\).

\end{fact}

\hypertarget{undergrad-review}{%
\subsection{Undergrad Review}\label{undergrad-review}}

\begin{remark}

Notation:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\left\langle{ a }\right\rangle \coloneqq Ra \coloneqq\left\{{ ra {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}r \in R }\right\}\)
  is the ideal generated by a single element.
\item
  \(R = \left\langle{ 1 }\right\rangle\) is equivalently the ideal
  generated by 1.
\end{itemize}

\end{remark}

\hypertarget{basics}{%
\subsubsection{Basics}\label{basics}}

\begin{definition}[Ring]

A \textbf{ring} is a triple \((R, +, \cdot) \in \mathsf{CRing}\) such
that

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \((R, +)\in {\mathsf{Ab}}{\mathsf{Grp}}\),
\item
  \((R, \cdot) \in \mathsf{Mon}\)
\item
  Distributivity: \(a(b+c) = ab + ac\).
\end{itemize}

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[of rings]

Some of the most important examples of rings:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The usual suspects: \({\mathbb{Z}}, {\mathbb{Q}}\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Their analogs: number fields \(K \coloneqq{\mathbb{Q}}(\zeta)\),
    their rings of integers \({\mathbb{Z}}_K\) or \({\mathcal{O}}_K\),
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Gaussian integers \({\mathbb{Z}}(i)\)
\item
  Fields \(k = {\mathbb{F}_{p^n}}, {\mathbb{R}}\)
\item
  Fraction fields of rings \(\operatorname{ff}(R)\),
  e.g.~\(\operatorname{ff}({\mathbb{Z}}) = {\mathbb{Q}}\).
\item
  Polynomial rings
  \(R { \left[ {{ {x}_1, {x}_2, \cdots, {x}_{n}}} \right] }\),
  particularly for \(R=k\) a field
\item
  Power series rings
  \(R { \left[ {{ {x}_1, {x}_2, \cdots, {x}_{n}}} \right] }\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Formal power series rings
    \(R { \left[\left[ {{ {x}_1, {x}_2, \cdots, {x}_{n}}} \right] \right] }\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \({ {\mathbb{Z}}_p }\coloneqq\left\{{a/b {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}p \notdivides b }\right\}\)
  the ring of \(p{\hbox{-}}\)adic integers
\item
  Rings of germs, e.g.~\(C^\infty(X, Y)\) where \(f\sim g\) iff there
  exists some \(U \subseteq X\) with
  \({ \left.{{f}} \right|_{{U}} } = { \left.{{g}} \right|_{{U}} }\).
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\begin{definition}[Ring Morphism]

A morphism \(f\in \mathsf{CRing}(X, Y)\) satisfies:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(f(1_X) = 1_Y\)
\item
  \(f(a(b+c)) = f(a)f(b) + f(a)f(c)\)
\end{itemize}

\end{definition}

\begin{remark}

Important notes:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\ker f \coloneqq f^{-1}(\left\{{0}\right\})\).
\item
  A bijective ring morphisms is automatically an isomorphism in
  \(\mathsf{CRing}\).
\item
  \(\ker f {~\trianglelefteq~}X\) is an ideal, but
  \(\operatorname{im}f \leq Y\) is only a subring in general.
\item
  For any ideal \(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\) there is a quotient map
  \(R\to R/I\), it's useful to write cosets as \(a+I\).
\item
  For quotients, \(x \equiv y \operatorname{mod}I \iff x-y \in I\).
\end{itemize}

\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Ideal]

An \textbf{ideal} \(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\) is a subset where
\((I, +) \leq (R, +) \in {\mathsf{Grp}}\) is a subgroup and for
\(x\in R, i\in I\), \(xi \in I\). Equivalently,

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(RI \subseteq I\)
\item
  \(I + I \subseteq I\)
\end{itemize}

Note that \(0\) is in every ideal.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Characteristic]

Using that every ring has a
\({\mathsf{{\mathbb{Z}}}{\hbox{-}}\mathsf{Mod}}\) structure, the
\textbf{characteristic} of a ring \(R\) is the smallest \(n\) such that
\(n\curvearrowright 1_R = 0_R\), i.e.~\(\sum_{i=1}^n 1_R = 0_R\).

\end{definition}

\hypertarget{elements}{%
\subsubsection{Elements}\label{elements}}

\begin{definition}[Divisibility of Elements]

An element \(r\in R\) is \textbf{divisible} by \(q \in R\) if and only
if there exists some \(c \in R\) such that \(r = qc\). In this case, we
sometimes write \(q\divides r\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Units]

An element \(r\in R\) is a \textbf{unit} if \(r\divides 1\): there
exists an \(s\in R\) such that \(rs = sr = 1\). Then
\(r^{-1}\coloneqq s\) is uniquely determined, and the set of units
\((R^{\times}, \cdot) \in {\mathsf{Ab}}{\mathsf{Grp}}\) forms a group.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Irreducible Element]

An element \(r\in R\) is \textbf{irreducible} iff
\begin{align*}
r=ab \implies a \in R^{\times}\text{ or }  b\in R^{\times}
\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Prime Element]

An element \(p\in R\) is \textbf{prime} iff
\begin{align*}  
a,b \in R^{\times}\setminus\left\{{0}\right\}, \quad ab\divides p \implies a\divides p \text{ or }  b\divides p
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{fact}

If \(R\) is an integral domain, prime \(\implies\) irreducible. If \(R\)
is a UFD, then irreducible \(\implies\) prime, so this is an iff.

\end{fact}

\begin{definition}[Associate Elements]

\(a, b\in R\) are \textbf{associates} iff there exists a
\(u\in R^{\times}\) such that \(a = ub\). Equivalently, \(a\divides b\)
and \(b\divides a\).

\end{definition}

\hypertarget{ideals}{%
\subsubsection{Ideals}\label{ideals}}

\begin{example}[of specs]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\operatorname{Id}({\mathbb{Z}}) = \left\{{ \left\langle{m}\right\rangle {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}m \in {\mathbb{Z}}^{\geq 0}}\right\}\)
\item
  \(\operatorname{mSpec}{\mathbb{Z}}= \left\{{ \left\langle{p}\right\rangle {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}p\neq 0 \text{ is prime} }\right\}\)
\item
  \(\operatorname{Spec}{\mathbb{Z}}= \operatorname{mSpec}{\mathbb{Z}}\cup\left\{{ \left\langle{0}\right\rangle }\right\}\).
\item
  For \(k\) a field and \(f\in k[x_1, \cdots, x_{n}]\) irreducible,
  \(\left\langle{f}\right\rangle \in \operatorname{Spec}k[x_1, \cdots, x_{n}]\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \({\mathfrak{m}}\coloneqq\left\{{ f = \sum_I a_I x^I\in k[x_1, \cdots, x_{n}]{~\mathrel{\Big|}~}a_0 = 0 }\right\} \in \operatorname{mSpec}k[x_1, \cdots, x_{n}]\)
    (i.e.~this is the ideal of polynomials with no constant term).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\begin{proposition}[Proper ideals contain no units]

If \(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\) is a proper ideal \(\iff I\) contains no
units.

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}

\(r\in R^{\times}\cap I \implies r^{-1}r \in I \implies 1\in I \implies x\cdot 1 \in I \quad \forall x\in R\).

\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}

If \(I_1 \subseteq I_2 \subseteq \cdots\) are ideals then \(\cup_j I_j\)
is an ideal.

\end{proposition}

\begin{definition}[Irreducible Ideal]

An ideal \(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\) is \textbf{irreducible} if it can not
be written as the intersection of two larger ideals, i.e.~there are not
\(J_1, J_2 \supseteq I\) such that \(J_1 \cap J_2 = I\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Prime Ideal]

\({\mathfrak{p}}\) is a \textbf{prime} ideal \(\iff\)
\begin{align*} 
ab\in {\mathfrak{p}}\implies a\in {\mathfrak{p}}\text{ or } b\in {\mathfrak{p}}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Prime implies irreducible for UFDs]

In \(R\) a UFD, an element \(r\in R\) is prime \(\iff r\) is
irreducible.

\end{proposition}

\begin{example}[of why the converse doesn't hold]

For \(R\) an integral domain, prime \(\implies\) irreducible, but
generally not the converse. Take
\(R \coloneqq k[x, y]/\left\langle{x^2-y^3}\right\rangle \cong k[x^2, y^3]\),
which is a domain, But here \([x^2] = [y^3]\) as equivalence classes
where \([y^3]\) is irreducible since every element in \(r\in R\) has
\(\deg_y(r) = 0,3,6,\cdots\). But \([y^3]\) is not prime since it
divides \([x^2]\) but doesn't divide \([x]\).

\end{example}

\begin{definition}[Prime Spectrum]

The \textbf{prime spectrum} (or just the \textbf{spectrum}) of \(R\) is
defined as
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{Spec}(R) = \left\{{{\mathfrak{p}}{~\trianglelefteq~}R {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}{\mathfrak{p}}\text{ is prime}}\right\}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Maximal Ideal]

An ideal \({\mathfrak{m}}\) is \textbf{maximal} iff whenever
\(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\) with \({\mathfrak{m}}\subsetneq I\) a proper
containment then \(I = R\).

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[Some counterexamples]

Some examples. Reminder: maximal always implies prime, and for PIDs,
prime \emph{and nonzero} implies maximal. Maximals quotient to fields,
primes to domains.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Prime and maximal:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(p{\mathbb{Z}}\in \operatorname{Id}({\mathbb{Z}})\). Maximal (and
    thus prime) since \({\mathbb{Z}}/p\) is a field and a domain.
  \item
    \(\left\langle{2, x}\right\rangle \in \operatorname{Id}({\mathbb{Z}}[x])\).
    Maximal (and thus prime)
    \({\mathbb{Z}}[x]/\left\langle{2, x}\right\rangle \cong {\mathbb{Z}}/2\)
    is a field and a domain.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Prime but not maximal:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(\left\langle{0}\right\rangle \in \operatorname{Id}({\mathbb{Z}})\),
    since \(m{\mathbb{Z}}\supseteq\left\langle{0}\right\rangle\) for any
    \(m\).
  \item
    \(\left\langle{x}\right\rangle \in R[x]\) over any integral domain
    since \(R[x]/\left\langle{x}\right\rangle \cong R\) is a domain
    (making it maximal), but \(R\) can be chosen not to be a field
    (making it non-prime).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Not prime, not maximal:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(m{\mathbb{Z}}\in \operatorname{Id}({\mathbb{Z}})\), since \(m\)
    composite implies \({\mathbb{Z}}/m\) is not a domain since it has
    nonzero zero divisors. For example, in \({\mathbb{Z}}/6\), \([3]\)
    is a zero divisors since \([2][3] = 0\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Useful examples:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(\operatorname{mSpec}{\mathbb{Z}}= \left\{{p{\mathbb{Z}}}\right\}\)
    and
    \(\operatorname{Spec}{\mathbb{Z}}= \left\{{p{\mathbb{Z}}}\right\} \cup\left\langle{0}\right\rangle\).
  \item
    \(\operatorname{mSpec}{\mathbb{C}}[x] = \left\{{x-a {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}a\in {\mathbb{C}}}\right\}\),
    since over a PID \(\left\langle{\alpha}\right\rangle\) is maximal
    iff \(\alpha\) is irreducible, and over \({\mathbb{C}}\)
    irreducibles are degree 1.
  \item
    \(\operatorname{mSpec}k[x_1, \cdots, x_{n}] = \left\{{\left\langle{x-a_1, x-a_2, \cdots, x-a_n}\right\rangle {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}a_k \in k}\right\}\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A ring with no maximal ideals: the PrÃ¼fer \(p{\hbox{-}}\)group
  \({\mathbb{Z}}(p^\infty) = \left\{{\zeta_{p^k}}\right\}_{k=1}^{\infty}\)
  with the trivial ring structure \(xy = 0\). The subgroups are
  \(H_k \coloneqq\left\{{\zeta_{p^k}}\right\}\), which form an
  increasing chain that doesn't stabilize.
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\begin{definition}[Max Spectrum]

The \textbf{max spectrum} of \(R\) is defined as
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{mSpec}(R) = \left\{{{\mathfrak{m}}{~\trianglelefteq~}R {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}{\mathfrak{m}}\text{ is maximal}}\right\}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[An irreducible element that is not prime.]

\(3\in {\mathbb{Z}}[\sqrt{-5}]\). Check norm to see irreducibility, but
\(3 \divides 9 = (2+\sqrt{-5})(2-\sqrt{-5})\) and doesn't divide either
factor.

\end{example}

\begin{example}

Maximal ideals of \(R[x]\) are of the form \(I = (x - a_i)\) for some
\(a_i \in R\).

\end{example}

\hypertarget{types-of-rings}{%
\subsection{Types of Rings}\label{types-of-rings}}

\begin{definition}[Division ring or skew field]

A \textbf{division ring} is any (potentially noncommutative) ring \(R\)
for which \(R\setminus\left\{{0}\right\}\subset {\mathbb{R}}^{\times}\),
i.e.~every nonzero element is a unit and thus has a multiplicative
inverse.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Zero Divisor]

An element \(r\in R\) is a \textbf{zero-divisor} iff there exists an
\(a\in R\setminus\left\{{0}\right\}\) such that \(ar = ra = 0\),
i.e.~\(r\divides 0\). Equivalently, the map
\begin{align*}  
r\cdot: R &\to R \\
x &\mapsto rx
\end{align*}
fails to be injective.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Integral Domain]

A ring is an \textbf{integral domain} if and only if it has no nonzero
zero divisors:
\begin{align*}  
a, b\in R\setminus\left\{{0}\right\}, ab = 0 \implies a = 0 {\operatorname{ { or } }} b = 0
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[of integral domains]

Examples of integral domains:
\({\mathbb{Z}}, k { \left[ {{ {x}_1, {x}_2, \cdots, {x}_{n}}} \right] }\).
Non-examples: \({\mathbb{Z}}/6, \operatorname{Mat}(2\times 2; k)\)

\end{example}

\begin{definition}[Field]

A \textbf{field} is a commutative division ring, i.e.~every nonzero
element is a uni, i.e.~every nonzero element is a unit

\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that TFAE:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(A\in \mathsf{Field}\)
\item
  \(A\) is a simple ring, so
  \(\operatorname{Id}(A) = \left\{{ 0, A }\right\}\).
\item
  If \(B\in \mathsf{Field}\) is nonzero then every ring morphism
  \(A\to B\) is injective.
\end{itemize}

\end{exercise}

\begin{remark}

Every field is an integral domain, but e.g.~\({\mathbb{Z}}\) is an
integral domain that is not a field.

\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Principal Ideal]

An ideal \(I {~\trianglelefteq~}R\) if \textbf{principal} if there
exists an \(a\in R\) such that \(I = \left\langle{a}\right\rangle\),
i.e.~\(I = Ra\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Principal Ideal Domain]

A ring \(R\) is a \textbf{principal ideal domain} iff every ideal is
principal.

\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that if \(R\) is a PID then
\(\operatorname{Spec}R \subseteq \operatorname{mSpec}R\).

\end{exercise}

\begin{definition}[Unique Factorization Domain]

A ring \(R\) is a \textbf{unique factorization domain} iff \(R\) is an
integral domain and every \(r\in R\setminus\left\{{0}\right\}\) admits a
decomposition
\begin{align*}  
r = u \mfpod_{i=1}^n p_i
\end{align*}
where \(u\in R^{\times}\) and the \(p_i\) irreducible, which is unique
up to associates.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Noetherian]

A ring \(R\) is \textbf{Noetherian} if the ACC holds: every ascending
chain of ideals \(I_1 \leq I_2 \cdots\) stabilizes in the sense that
there exists some \(N\) such that \(I_N = I_{N+1} = \cdots\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Reduced Ring]

A ring \(R\) is \textbf{reduced} if \(R\) contains no nonzero nilpotent
elements.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Local Ring]

A ring \(R\) is \textbf{local} iff it contains a unique maximal ideal
\({\mathfrak{m}}\), so
\(\operatorname{mSpec}R = \left\{{ 0, {\mathfrak{m}}}\right\}\). As a
consequence, there is a uniquely associated \textbf{residue field}
\(\kappa \coloneqq R/{\mathfrak{m}}\).

\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that if \(R\) is a nonzero ring where every element is either a
unit or nilpotent, then \(R\) is local.

\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that if \(p\in \operatorname{Spec}R\) then
\(R \left[ { \scriptstyle p^{-1}} \right]\) is local.

\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Suppose \({\mathfrak{m}}\in \operatorname{mSpec}R\) is a proper maximal
ideal. Show that under either of the following two conditions, \(R\) is
local:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(R\setminus{\mathfrak{m}}\subseteq R^{\times}\), so every element of
  \(R\setminus{\mathfrak{m}}\) is a unit, or
\item
  \(1 + {\mathfrak{m}}\subseteq R^{\times}\)
\end{itemize}

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Sketch: \({\mathfrak{m}}\) must contain every non-unit.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If \(I \neq R\) then \(I\) contains no units, so
    \(I\subseteq N \coloneqq R\setminus R^{\times}\), i.e.~\(I\) is
    contained in the non-units. But \(N \subseteq {\mathfrak{m}}\) since
    no element of \({\mathfrak{m}}\) is a unit and no element of
    \(R\setminus{\mathfrak{m}}\) is a non-unit.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Sketch: show that every \(r\in R\setminus{\mathfrak{m}}\) is a unit
  and apply the first part.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If \(r\in R\setminus{\mathfrak{m}}\) then
    \(\left\langle{r, {\mathfrak{m}}}\right\rangle = R = \left\langle{ 1 }\right\rangle\)
    so \(rt + m = 1\) for some \(t\in R, m\in {\mathfrak{m}}\), so
    \(rt = 1-m \in 1 + {\mathfrak{m}}\subseteq R^{\times}\) by
    assumption. Now apply (1).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{solution}

\begin{definition}[Dedekind Domains]

A \textbf{Dedekind domain} is an integral domain for which the monoid
\(\operatorname{Id}(R)\) of nonzero ideals of \(R\) satisfies unique
factorization: every ideal can be decomposed uniquely into a product of
prime ideals.

\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that a Dedekind domain \(R\) is a PID iff \(R\) is a UFD.

\end{exercise}

\begin{definition}[Valuation Ring]

A \textbf{valuation ring} is an integral domain \(R\) such that for
every \(x\in \operatorname{ff}(R)\), \(x\in R\) or \(x^{-1}\in R\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Discrete Valuation Rings]

A \textbf{discrete valuation ring} or \textbf{DVR} is a local PID with a
\emph{unique} maximal ideal.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Regular ring]

A commutative ring \(R\) is \textbf{regular} if \(R\) is Noetherian and
for every \(p\in \operatorname{Spec}R\) the localization
\(R \left[ { \scriptstyle p^{-1}} \right]\) is a \emph{regular local
ring}: it has a maximal ideal \({\mathfrak{m}}\) which admits a minimal
generating set of \(n\) elements where \(n\) is the Krull dimension of
\(R \left[ { \scriptstyle p^{-1}} \right]\).

\end{definition}

\begin{remark}

Motivation: if \(R = {\mathcal{O}}_{X, x}\) is the ring of germs at
\(x\) of an algebraic variety \(X\), then \(R\) is regular iff \(X\) is
nonsingular at \(x\).

\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Gorenstein Rings]

A commutative Noetherian ring \(R\) is \textbf{Gorenstein} iff \(R\)
viewed as an \(R{\hbox{-}}\)module has finite injective dimension.

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[Why care about Gorenstein rings?]

If \(R\in {\mathsf{gr}\,}{\mathsf{Alg}_{/k} }\) with
\(\dim_k R < \infty\), then \(R\) decomposes as
\(R = R_0 \oplus R_1 \oplus \cdots R_n\) with \(R_0 \coloneqq k\), and
\(R\) is Gorenstein iff \(R\) satisfies ``PoincarÃ© duality'':
\(\dim_k R_0 = \dim_k R_m = 1\) and there is a perfect pairing
\(R_i \otimes_k R_{n-j} \to R_n\).

\end{example}

\hypertarget{comparing-and-transporting-ring-types}{%
\subsection{Comparing and Transporting Ring
Types}\label{comparing-and-transporting-ring-types}}

\begin{proposition}[Big chain of inclusions]

Fields \(\subset\) Euclidean domains \(\subset\) PIDs \(\subset\) UFDs
\(\subset\) Integral Domains \(\subset\) Rings

\end{proposition}

\todo[inline]{Prove inclusions}

\begin{proposition}[PID implies UFD]

If \(R\) is a PID, then every element in \(R\) has a unique prime
factorization.

\end{proposition}

\begin{example}[showing these inclusions are strict]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A Euclidean Domain that is not a field: \({\mathbb{F}}[x]\) for
  \({\mathbb{F}}\) a field

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Proof}: Use previous lemma, and \(x\) is not invertible
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A PID that is not a Euclidean Domain:
  \({\mathbb{Z}}\left[\frac{1 + \sqrt{-19}}{2}\right]\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Proof}: complicated.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A UFD that is not a PID: \({\mathbb{F}}[x, y]\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Proof}: \(\left\langle{x, y}\right\rangle\) is not principal
  \end{itemize}
\item
  An integral domain that is not a UFD: \({\mathbb{Z}}[\sqrt{-5}]\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Proof}: \((2+\sqrt{-5})(2-\sqrt{-5})=9=3\cdot 3\), where all
    factors are irreducible (check norm).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A ring that is not an integral domain: \({\mathbb{Z}}/(4)\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Proof}: \(2 \operatorname{mod}4\) is a zero divisor.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\begin{proposition}[Polynomial rings over fields have division]

If \({\mathbb{F}}\) a field then \({\mathbb{F}}[x]\) is a Euclidean
domain.

\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Characterizations of Rings]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(R\) a commutative division ring \(\implies R\) is a field
\item
  \(R\) a finite integral domain \(\implies R\) is a field.
\item
  \({\mathbb{F}}\) a field \(\iff {\mathbb{F}}[x]\) is a PID.
\item
  \({\mathbb{F}}\) is a field \(\iff {\mathbb{F}}\) is a commutative
  simple ring.
\item
  \(R\) is a UFD \(\iff R[x]\) is a UFD.
\item
  \(R\) a PID \(\implies R[x]\) is a UFD
\item
  \(R\) a PID \(\implies R\) Noetherian
\item
  \(R[x]\) a PID \(\implies R\) is a field.
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{example}[?]

A polynomial ring over a PID is not necessarily a PID: take
\(\left\langle{2, x}\right\rangle {~\trianglelefteq~}{\mathbb{Z}}[x]\).

\end{example}

\hypertarget{radicals}{%
\subsection{Radicals}\label{radicals}}

\begin{definition}[Radical of an Ideal]

For an ideal \(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\), the \textbf{radical}
\begin{align*}
\sqrt{I} \coloneqq\left\{{r\in R{~\mathrel{\Big|}~}r^n\in I\text{ for some } n\geq 0}\right\}
,\end{align*}
so \(x^n \in I \implies x\in \sqrt{I}\).

\end{definition}

\begin{remark}

In general, ``radical'' refers to ``bad elements'' of some type to be
quotiented out, not necessarily \(\sqrt{{-}}\).

\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Radical Ideal]

An ideal is \textbf{radical} iff \(\sqrt{I} = I\).

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Nilpotent]

An element \(r\in R\) is \textbf{nilpotent} if \(r^n = 0\) for some
\(n \in {\mathbb{Z}}^{\geq 0}\).

\end{definition}

\begin{fact}

The binomial expansion works in any ring:
\begin{align*}
(a+b)^n = \sum_{k\leq n} {n\choose k} a^k b^{n-k}
.\end{align*}

This is useful when considering nilpotents or radicals.

\end{fact}

\begin{exercise}[?]

\envlist

Notation: let \(N\) or \(N(R)\) be the set of nilpotents in \(R\). Let
\(ZD\) or \(ZD(R)\) be the set of zero divisors. Let
\(U, U(R), R^{\times}\) be the units of \(R\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Show that every nilpotent is either zero or a zero divisor.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Solution: \(a^m=0\) with \(a\neq 0\) and \(m>1\), then
    \(x x^{m-1} = 0\), so \(x^{m-1}\) is a nontrivial element
    annihilating \(x\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Show that \(R\) commutative and unital and \(x\) nilpotent implies
  \(1+x\) is a unit, and moreover \(N + R^{\times}= R^{\times}\) (the
  sum of a nilpotent and unit is a unit).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Solution: expand
    \(1/(1+x) = \sum_{k=0}^\infty (-x)^k = \sum_{k=0}^n (-x)^k \coloneqq f(x)\),
    so \((1+x)f(x) =1\). Now use that \(RN = N\) since \(x^n=0\) implies
    \((rx)^n = rxrx\cdots rx = r^n x^n = 0\). Taking
    \(n + u\in N + R^{\times}\), then
    \(u+n = u^{-1}(1 + u^{-1}n) \in R^{\times}R^{\times}\) since
    \(u^{-1}n\in N\) and \(1+u^{-1}\in R^{\times}\) by the first part.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Show that \(f(x) = \sum a_k x^k \in R[x]\) iff
  \(f\in R[x]^{\times}\iff a_0\in R^{\times}, a_{k>1}\in N\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Solution: use that if \(a_k\) is nilpotent, \(a_k x^k\) is
    nilpotent. Then \(a_0\) a unit at \(a_1 x\) nilpotent implies
    \(a_0 + a_1 x\) is a unit, and inductively \(f\) is a unit. If \(f\)
    is a unit, take \(fg=1\) with \(f = \sum_{k=0}^na_k x^k\) and
    \(g = \sum_{k=0}^m a_k x^k\). Write
    \(fg(x) = \sum_{k=0}^{n+m} c_k x^k\) where
    \(c_k = \sum_{j=0}^k a_j b_{k-j}\). Using \(fg=1\),
    \(c_{0} = a_0 b_0 = 1\) so \(a_0, b_0\) are units, and proceed
    inductively by descending coefficients, checking that \(a_n b_m\) is
    the \(r=0\) case.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Show that \(f(x) \in N(R[x]) \iff a_k \in N(R)\) for all \(k\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Solution: \(f\) nilpotent with \(f(x) = \sum a_k x^k\) implies
    \(f^m=0\), and check the leading term \(a_n^m x^{nm}\). Induct down:
    \(f, a_nx^n\) nilpotent implies \(f - a_n x^n\) nilpotent.
    Conversely, if \(a_i^{n_i} = 0\), use that
    \(N(R) {~\trianglelefteq~}R\) form an ideal.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Show that \(f\in ZD(R[x]) \iff f\neq 0\) and \(rf(x) = 0\) for some
  \(r\in R\).
\end{itemize}

\end{exercise}

\begin{definition}[Nilradical]

The \textbf{nilradical} of \(R \in \mathsf{CRing}\) is
\begin{align*}
{\sqrt{0_{R}} } \coloneqq\left\{{x\in R {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}x \text{ is nilpotent}}\right\} = \sqrt{0}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[Quotient by nilradical is reduced]

Show \({\sqrt{0_{R}} } {~\trianglelefteq~}R\) is an ideal and
\(A/{\sqrt{0_{R}} }\) is reduced.

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(R{\sqrt{0_{R}} } \subseteq R\): For \(r\) nilpotent of order \(n\)
  and \(x\in R\), \(xr\) is nilpotent since
  \begin{align*}
  (xr)^n = (xr)(xr)\cdots(xr) = x^n r^n = x^n 0 = 0
  .\end{align*}
\item
  \(R^2 \subseteq R\), for \(r,s\in {\sqrt{0_{R}} }\) write
  \(r^{n} = s^m = 0\), then
  \begin{align*}
  (r+s)^{N} = \sum_{k\geq 0}{N \choose k} r^k s^{N-k}
  ,\end{align*}
  so just choose \(N\) large enough so that either \(k>n\) or \(N-k> m\)
  always holds, e.g.~\(N\coloneqq n+m-1\).
\item
  \(R/{\sqrt{0_{R}} }\) has no nonzero nilpotents: Take
  \(\mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5mur\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu \in R/{\sqrt{0_{R}} }\)
  for some \(r\in R\), then
  \(\phi(r^n) = \phi(r)^n = \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5mur\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu^n\).
  So
  \begin{align*}
  \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5mur\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu^n=0 \operatorname{mod}{\sqrt{0_{R}} } \iff \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5mur^n\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu \equiv 0 \operatorname{mod}{\sqrt{0_{R}} } \iff r^n \in {\sqrt{0_{R}} } \iff r\in 0_R
  .\end{align*}
\end{itemize}

\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that the nilradical is the intersection of all prime ideals.

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

\begin{quote}
See A\&M 1.8
\end{quote}

Write \(P\) as the intersection of all prime ideals of \(R\).

\({\sqrt{0_{R}} } \subseteq P\): Suppose \(r\in {\sqrt{0_{R}} }\) so
\(r^n = 0\) and let \({\mathfrak{p}}\in \operatorname{Spec}R\). Then use
that \(0\in I\) for any ideal:
\(r^n = 0 \in {\mathfrak{p}}\implies r\in {\mathfrak{p}}\) since
\({\mathfrak{p}}\) is prime.

\({\sqrt{0_{R}} }^c \subseteq P^c\): Fix \(f\) non-nilpotent, we want to
show \(f\) is not in any prime ideal. set \(S \subseteq R\) to be all
ideals \(I\) such that \(f^{>0} \not \in I\). Apply Zorn's lemma:
\(S\neq \emptyset\) since \(0\in S\), so after ordering \(I\) by
inclusions \(S\) contains a maximal \({\mathfrak{p}}\) which we claim is
prime. If \(a,b \in {\mathfrak{p}}^c\) then
\({\mathfrak{p}}+ \left\langle{ a }\right\rangle\) and
\({\mathfrak{p}}+ \left\langle{b}\right\rangle supset {\mathfrak{p}}\)
strictly, and by maximality they aren't in \(S\). So there exist \(m,n\)
such that \(f^m\in {\mathfrak{p}}+ \left\langle{ a }\right\rangle\) and
\(f^n \in {\mathfrak{p}}+ \left\langle{b}\right\rangle\). Then
\(f^{m+n} \in {\mathfrak{p}}+ \left\langle{ab}\right\rangle\), so
\({\mathfrak{p}}+ \left\langle{ab}\right\rangle\) is not in \(S\). Thus
\(ab\not \in {\mathfrak{p}}\) so \(f\not\in {\mathfrak{p}}\). Letting
\({\mathfrak{p}}\) be arbitrary yields \(f\not \in P\).

\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that
\({\sqrt{0_{R}} } = \displaystyle\bigcap_{p\in \operatorname{Spec}R} p\)
is the intersection of all prime ideals \(p{~\trianglelefteq~}R\).

\end{exercise}

\begin{definition}[Jacobson Radical]

The \textbf{Jacobson radical} \({\sqrt{0_{R}} }\) is the intersection of
all maximal ideals, i.e.
\begin{align*}  
{\sqrt{0_{R}} } = \displaystyle\bigcap_{{\mathfrak{m}}\in \operatorname{mSpec}R} {\mathfrak{m}}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show \(x\in {\sqrt{0_{R}} } \iff 1-xR \subseteq R^{\times}\).

\end{exercise}

\hypertarget{structure-theorems}{%
\subsection{Structure Theorems}\label{structure-theorems}}

\begin{definition}[Simple Modules]

A module \(M\) is \textbf{simple} iff every submodule \(M' \leq M\) is
either \(0\) or \(M\). A ring \(R\) is simple if and only if it is
simple as an \(R{\hbox{-}}\)module, i.e.~there are no nontrivial proper
ideals.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Semisimple Modules]

A module \(M\) is \textbf{simple} if and only if it admits a
decomposition
\begin{align*}
M = \bigoplus_{j\in J} M_j
\end{align*}
with each \(M_j\) simple.

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Krull]

Every ring has a proper maximal ideal, and any proper ideal is contained
in a maximal ideal.

\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Artin-Wedderburn?]

If \(R\) is a nonzero, unital, \emph{semisimple} ring then
\begin{align*}
R \cong \bigoplus_{i=1}^m \mathrm{Mat}(n_i, D_i)
,\end{align*}
a finite sum of matrix rings over division rings.

\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}

If \(M\) is a simple ring over \(R\) a division ring, the \(M\) is
isomorphic to a matrix ring.

\end{corollary}

\begin{theorem}[Wedderburn]

Every finite division ring is a field, i.e.~finite division rings must
be commutative.

\end{theorem}

\hypertarget{zorns-lemma}{%
\subsection{Zorn's Lemma}\label{zorns-lemma}}

\begin{definition}[Chain in a poset]

In a poset, a \textbf{chain} is a totally ordered subset. An
\textbf{upper bound} on a subset \(S\) of a poset \(X\) is any
\(x\in X\) such that \(s\leq x\) for all \(s\in S\).

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Zorn's Lemma]

If \(P\) is a poset in which every chain has an upper bound, then \(P\)
has a maximal element.

\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}

You can always form a subset poset, and restrict with any sub-collection
thereof with a set predicate. To use Zorn's lemma, you need to take an
arbitrary chain in your poset \(X\), produce an upper bound \(U\)
(e.g.~by taking a union), and showing that \(U\) is still in \(X\)
(i.e.~it still satisfies the right predicate).

\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Existence of maximal ideals]

Every proper ideal is contained in a maximal ideal.

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}

Let \(0 < I < R\) be a proper ideal, and consider the set
\begin{align*}
S = \left\{{J {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}I   \subseteq J < R}\right\}
.\end{align*}

Note \(I\in S\), so \(S\) is nonempty. The claim is that \(S\) contains
a maximal element \(M\).

\(S\) is a poset, ordered by set inclusion, so if we can show that every
chain has an upper bound, we can apply Zorn's lemma to produce \(M\).

Let \(C \subseteq S\) be a chain in \(S\), so
\(C = \left\{{C_1 \subseteq C_2 \subseteq \cdots}\right\}\) and define
\(\widehat{C} = \cup_i C_i\).

\textbf{\(\widehat{C}\) is an upper bound for \(C\):} This follows
because every \(C_i \subseteq \widehat{C}\).

\textbf{\(\widehat{C}\) is in \(S\):} Use the fact that
\(I \subseteq C_i < R\) for every \(C_i\) and since no \(C_i\) contains
a unit, \(\widehat{C}\) doesn't contain a unit, and is thus proper.

\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that every non-unit of \(R\) is contained in a maximal ideal.

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

This follows because if \(x\in R\setminus R^{\times}\), then
\(Rx {~\trianglelefteq~}R\) and \(Rx\neq R\) implies \(R/Rx \neq 0\).
Then there exists some
\(\mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5mu{\mathfrak{m}}\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu\in \operatorname{mSpec}R/Rx\),
and by the correspondence theorem this lifts to some
\({\mathfrak{m}}\in \operatorname{mSpec}R\) containing \(Rx\).

\end{solution}

\hypertarget{unsorted}{%
\subsection{Unsorted}\label{unsorted}}

\begin{fact}

Division algorithm for Euclidean domains.

\todo[inline]{todo}

\end{fact}

\begin{definition}[Field of fractions]

For \(R\in \mathsf{CRing}\) an integral domain, the \textbf{field of
fractions} of \(R\) can be constructed as
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{ff}(R) \coloneqq\qty{R \times R^{\bullet}}/\sim && (a,s)\sim bt \iff at-bs = 0_R
.\end{align*}

\begin{quote}
Checking transitivity requires having no nonzero zero divisors.
\end{quote}

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Localization]

For \(R\in \mathsf{CRing}\) and \(S \subseteq R\) a
\emph{multiplicatively closed} subset, so \(RS \subseteq S\) and
\(1_R\in S\), the \textbf{localization of \(R\) at \(S\)} can be
constructed as
\begin{align*}
R \left[ { \scriptstyle S^{-1}} \right] \coloneqq\qty{R\times S} / \sim && (a, s)\sim (b, t) \iff \exists u\in S\quad (at-bs)u = 0_R
.\end{align*}

\begin{quote}
Why the \(u\): use in proof of transitivity.
\end{quote}

\end{definition}

\todo[inline]{Universal property.}

\begin{warnings}

There is a canonical ring morphism
\begin{align*}
R &\to R \left[ { \scriptstyle S^{-1}} \right] \\
x &\mapsto {x\over 1}
,\end{align*}
but this may not be injective.

\end{warnings}

\begin{remark}

For integral domains \(R\),
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{ff}(R) \cong R \left[ { \scriptstyle  (R^{\bullet}) ^{-1}} \right]
.\end{align*}

\end{remark}

\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}[Primary Ideal]

An ideal \(I{~\trianglelefteq~}R\) is \textbf{primary} iff whenever
\(pq\in I\), \(p\in I\) and \(q^n\in I\) for some \(n\).

\end{definition}

\end{proposition}

\begin{exercise}[?]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Show that in a PID, every element can be written as a finite product
  of irreducibles.
\item
  Show that in a PID, every maximal ideal is generated by an
  \emph{irreducible} element.
\item
  Show that any PID is Noetherian.
\item
  Show that not \({\mathbb{Z}}\) is Noetherian but not Artinian.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Hint: take a chain
    \(n{\mathbb{Z}}\supseteq n^2{\mathbb{Z}}\supseteq\cdots\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that \(R[x]\) a PID \(\iff R\) is a field.

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

Hint: take \(r\in R\), then
\(\left\langle{r, x}\right\rangle = \left\langle{f}\right\rangle\) for
some \(f\). Write \(r = fp\) and \(x = fq\) for \(p, q\in R[x]\), show
\(\deg f = 0\) and \(\deg q = 1\). Write \(f = c\) a constant,
\(q(x) = ax + b\) to get
\(c(ax+b)=x \implies ca=1 \implies c\in R^{\times}\implies \left\langle{f}\right\rangle = R[x]\).
Conclude by writing \(1= ar_1(x) + xr_2(x)\), evaluate at \(x=0\) to get
\(a^{-1}= r_1(0)\).

\end{solution}

\hypertarget{number-theory}{%
\section{Number Theory}\label{number-theory}}

\begin{proposition}[Properties of the norm]

Let \(K\) be a number field and \(N:K\to {\mathbb{Z}}\) be its norm
function.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(N(ab) = N(a)N(b)\)
\item
  \(a\divides b \in K \implies N(a)\divides N(b)\in {\mathbb{Z}}\).
\item
  \(a\in K^{\times}\iff N(a) = \pm 1\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\hypertarget{general-field-theory}{%
\section{General Field Theory}\label{general-field-theory}}

\begin{remark}

The most useful tricks of the trade:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\# {\mathbb{G}}_m({\mathbb{GF}}(p^k)) = p^k-1\), since every element
  is invertible except 0. You can use this to cook up strong numerical
  constraints on orders of elements. E.g. if \(a^{17}=1\) in some finite
  field of size \(p^k\), \(o(a)\) divides 17 and \(o(a)\) divides
  \(p^{k}-1\), so \(o(a)\) divides \(\gcd(17, p^{k}-1)\).
\item
  Multiplicativity in towers can force numerical divisibility
  constraints. E.g. if \(\alpha\) is a root of any irreducible \(f\),
  take the tower \(\operatorname{SF}(\alpha, k)/k(\alpha)/k\): then the
  degree of \(\min_{\alpha, k}(x)\in k[x]\) divides the degree of the
  extension \([\operatorname{SF}(\alpha, k) : k]\).
\item
\end{itemize}

\end{remark}

\hypertarget{basics-polynomials}{%
\subsection{Basics: Polynomials}\label{basics-polynomials}}

\begin{definition}[Reducible and Irreducible Polynomials]

For \({\mathbb{F}}\) a field, a polynomial \(f\in {\mathbb{F}}[x]\) is
\textbf{reducible} if and only if \(f\) can be factored as
\(f(x) = g(x) h(x)\) for some \(g, h\in {\mathbb{F}}[x]\) with
\(\deg g, \deg h \geq 1\) (so \(g, h\) are nonconstant). \(f\) is
\textbf{irreducible} if \(f\) is not reducible.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Primitive Polynomials]

For \(R\) a UFD, a polynomial \(p\in R[x]\) is \textbf{primitive} iff
the greatest common divisors of its coefficients is a unit.

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Gauss' Lemma]

Let \(R\) be a UFD and \(F\) its field of fractions. Then a primitive
\(p\in R[x]\) (so e.g.~\(p\) monic) is irreducible in \(R[x] \iff p\) is
irreducible in \(F[x]\).

More precisely, if \(p = AB\) is reducible in \(F[x]\), then there exist
\(r,s\in F\) such that \(rA, sB\in R[x]\) and \(p = (rA)(sB)\) is a
factorization in \(R[x]\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}

A primitive polynomial \(p\in {\mathbb{Q}}[x]\) is irreducible
\(\iff p\) is irreducible in \({\mathbb{Z}}[x]\).

\end{corollary}

\hypertarget{definitions-1}{%
\subsection{Definitions}\label{definitions-1}}

\begin{definition}[Characteristic]

The \textbf{characteristic} of a ring \(R\) is the smallest integer
\(p\) such that \(\sum_{k=1}^p 1 = 0\).

\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Freshman's Dream]

If \(\operatorname{ch}k = p\) then \((a+b)^p = a^p + b^p\) and
\((ab)^p = a^p b^p\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{definition}[Fixed Field]

For \(H \leq \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}_{{ \mathsf{Fields}_{k} }}(L)\),
\begin{align*}
L^H \coloneqq\left\{{ \ell \in L {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}\sigma(l) = \ell }\right\}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Prime Subfield]

The \textbf{prime subfield} of a field \(F\) is the subfield generated
by \(1\).

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Characterization of Prime Subfields]

The prime subfield of any field is isomorphic to either \({\mathbb{Q}}\)
or \({\mathbb{F}}_p\) for some \(p\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}[Field Automorphisms]

\begin{align*} 
\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(L/k) = \left\{{\sigma: L \to L {~\mathrel{\Big|}~} {\left.{{\sigma}} \right|_{{k}} }  = \operatorname{id}_k}\right\}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Embeddings and Lifts]

Let \(k\) denote a field, and \(L/k\) extension. Every field morphism is
an embedding (injection). An \textbf{embedding} of
\(k{\hbox{-}}\)algebras \(L\hookrightarrow L'\) will refer to any ring
morphism over \(k\), i.e.~a field morphism that restricts to the
identity on \(k\):

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzcd}
    L && {L'} \\
    \\
    & k
    \arrow[hook', from=3-2, to=1-1]
    \arrow[hook, from=3-2, to=1-3]
    \arrow[hook, from=1-1, to=1-3]
\end{tikzcd}
\end{center}

\begin{quote}
\href{https://q.uiver.app/?q=WzAsMyxbMCwwLCJMIl0sWzIsMCwiTCciXSxbMSwyLCJrIl0sWzIsMCwiIiwwLHsic3R5bGUiOnsidGFpbCI6eyJuYW1lIjoiaG9vayIsInNpZGUiOiJib3R0b20ifX19XSxbMiwxLCIiLDIseyJzdHlsZSI6eyJ0YWlsIjp7Im5hbWUiOiJob29rIiwic2lkZSI6InRvcCJ9fX1dLFswLDEsIiIsMCx7InN0eWxlIjp7InRhaWwiOnsibmFtZSI6Imhvb2siLCJzaWRlIjoidG9wIn19fV1d}{Link
to Diagram}
\end{quote}

More generally, we can ask for lifts of any map \(\sigma: k\to k'\):

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzcd}
    L && {L'} \\
    \\
    k && {k'}
    \arrow[""{name=0, anchor=center, inner sep=0}, hook, from=1-1, to=1-3]
    \arrow[""{name=1, anchor=center, inner sep=0}, "\sigma", hook, from=3-1, to=3-3]
    \arrow[hook, from=3-1, to=1-1]
    \arrow[hook, from=3-3, to=1-3]
    \arrow[shorten <=9pt, shorten >=9pt, Rightarrow, from=1, to=0]
\end{tikzcd}
\end{center}

\begin{quote}
\href{https://q.uiver.app/?q=WzAsNCxbMCwwLCJMIl0sWzIsMCwiTCciXSxbMCwyLCJrIl0sWzIsMiwia19cXHNpZ21hIl0sWzAsMSwiIiwwLHsic3R5bGUiOnsidGFpbCI6eyJuYW1lIjoiaG9vayIsInNpZGUiOiJ0b3AifX19XSxbMiwzLCJcXHNpZ21hIiwwLHsic3R5bGUiOnsidGFpbCI6eyJuYW1lIjoiaG9vayIsInNpZGUiOiJ0b3AifX19XSxbMiwwLCIiLDEseyJzdHlsZSI6eyJ0YWlsIjp7Im5hbWUiOiJob29rIiwic2lkZSI6InRvcCJ9fX1dLFszLDEsIiIsMSx7InN0eWxlIjp7InRhaWwiOnsibmFtZSI6Imhvb2siLCJzaWRlIjoidG9wIn19fV0sWzUsNCwiIiwwLHsic2hvcnRlbiI6eyJzb3VyY2UiOjIwLCJ0YXJnZXQiOjIwfX1dXQ==}{Link
to Diagram}
\end{quote}

Most often, we'll take \(\sigma: k\to k\) to be the identity.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Perfect Fields]

The following are equivalent:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(k\) is a \textbf{perfect} field.
\item
  If \(\operatorname{ch}k > 0\), the Frobenius is an automorphism of
  \(k\), so \(k^p = k\).
\item
  Every finite extension \(F/k\) is separable.
\item
  Every irreducible polynomial \(p\in k[x]\) is separable.
\end{itemize}

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[of a non-perfect field]

Example of a non-perfect field: \({\mathbb{F}}_p(t)\). Use that
\(f(x) \coloneqq x^p - t\) is irreducible in \({\mathbb{F}}_p(t)[x]\)
but not separable.

\end{example}

\begin{proposition}[Characterization of perfect fields]

\(k\) is perfect (using the irreducible implies separable condition) if
either

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\operatorname{ch}k = 0\) or
\item
  \(\operatorname{ch}k = p > 0\) and \(k^p = k\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[?]

For \(\operatorname{ch}k = 0\), use that irreducible implies separable.

For \(\operatorname{ch}k = p\), show that \(k_p\neq k \iff\) irreducible
does \emph{not} imply separable, so there exists an inseparable
irreducible.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Supposing \(k^p\neq k\), choose \(a\in k\) not a \(p\)th power.
\item
  Note that \(f(x) \coloneqq x^p-a\) has only one root in
  \(\mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muk\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu\): in a
  splitting field, any root \(r\) satisfies \(r^p=a\), so
  \begin{align*}
  x^p - a = x^p - r^p = (x-r)^p
  .\end{align*}
\item
  Note \(f\) is irreducible: its only possible divisors are \((x-r)^m\)
  for \(m \leq p\). Expanding yields
  \begin{align*}
  (x-r)^m = \sum_{k=0}^m {m\choose k} x^{m-k} (-r)^{k} = x^m + {m\choose 1} x^{m-1} (-r)^m + \cdots
  ,\end{align*}
  so the coefficient of \(x^{m-1}\) is \(-mr \in k\).
\item
  Thus if \((x-r)^m\) has a nontrivial divisor in \(k[x]\) then \(m\)
  must be in \(k^{\times}\), forcing \(r\in k\). But then
  \(r^p = a\in k\), \(\contradiction\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Numerical Invariants]

Let \(K/k\) be an extension.

\begin{align*}
[K: k] = \dim_{{ \mathsf{Vect} }_k} K
\end{align*}
is the dimension of \(K\) as a \(k{\hbox{-}}\)vector space.
Automorphisms of fields over \(K\) are defined as

\begin{align*} 
\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}_{{ \mathsf{Fields}_{k} }}(K) \coloneqq\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(K/k) \coloneqq\left\{{ \sigma: K \to K' {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}{ \left.{{\sigma}} \right|_{{k}} } = \operatorname{id}_k }\right\} 
, \end{align*}
so lifts of the identity on \(k\), and
\begin{align*}
\left\{{K:k}\right\} \coloneqq\# \mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(K/k)
.\end{align*}

If \(K/k\) is finite, normal, and separable,
\begin{align*}
{ \mathsf{Gal}} (K/k) \coloneqq\mathop{\mathrm{Aut}}(K/k)
,\end{align*}
where in general
\begin{align*}
\left\{{K: k}\right\} \leq [K: k]
\end{align*}
with equality when \(L/k\) is Galois.

\end{remark}

\begin{fact}

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  All fields are simple rings (no proper nontrivial ideals).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Thus every field morphism is either zero or injective.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The characteristic of any field \(k\) is either 0 or \(p\) a prime.
\item
  If \(L/k\) is algebraic, then \(\min(\alpha, L)\) divides
  \(\min(\alpha, k)\).
\end{itemize}

\end{fact}

\begin{proposition}[Towers are multiplicative in degree]

Let \(L/F/k\) be a finite tower of field extensions.
\begin{align*}
[L : k] = [L: F][F: k]
.\end{align*}

\end{proposition}

\hypertarget{finite-fields}{%
\subsection{Finite Fields}\label{finite-fields}}

\begin{theorem}[Construction of Finite Fields]

\({\mathbb{GF}}(p^n)\cong \frac{{\mathbb{F}}_p}{(f)}\) where
\(f \in {\mathbb{F}}_p[x]\) is any irreducible of degree \(n\), and
\({\mathbb{GF}}(p^n) \cong {\mathbb{F}}[\alpha] \cong {\operatorname{span}}_{\mathbb{F}}\left\{{1, \alpha, \cdots, \alpha^{n-1}}\right\}\)
for any root \(\alpha\) of \(f\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{proposition}[Prime Subfields of Finite Fields]

Every finite field \(F\) is isomorphic to a unique field of the form
\({\mathbb{GF}}(p^n)\) and if \(\operatorname{ch}F = p\), it has prime
subfield \({\mathbb{F}}_p\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Containment of Finite Fields]

\({\mathbb{GF}}(p^\ell) \leq {\mathbb{GF}}(p^k) \iff \ell\) divides
\(k\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Identification of Finite Fields as Splitting Fields]

\({\mathbb{GF}}(p^n)\) is the splitting field of
\(\rho(x) = x^{p^n} - x\), and the elements are exactly the roots of
\(\rho\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}

Every element is a root by Cauchy's theorem, and the \(p^n\) roots are
distinct since its derivative is identically \(-1\).

\todo[inline]{todo}

\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Splits Product of Irreducibles]

Let \(\rho_n \coloneqq x^{p^n} - x\). Then
\(f(x) \divides \rho_n(x) \iff \deg f \divides n\) and \(f\) is
irreducible.

\end{proposition}

\begin{corollary}

\(x^{p^n} - x = \prod f_i(x)\) over all irreducible monic
\(f_i \in {\mathbb{F}}_p[x]\) of degree \(d\) dividing \(n\).

\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}

\(\impliedby\):

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Suppose \(f\) is irreducible of degree \(d\).
\item
  Then \(f \divides x^{p^d} - x\), by considering
  \(F[x]/\left\langle{f}\right\rangle\).
\item
  Thus \(x^{p^d} - x \divides x^{p^n} - x \iff d\divides n\).
\end{itemize}

\(\implies\):

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\alpha \in {\mathbb{GF}}(p^n) \iff \alpha^{p^n} - \alpha = 0\), so
  every element is a root of \(\phi_n\) and
  \(\deg \min(\alpha, {\mathbb{F}}_p) \divides n\) since
  \({\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha)\) is an intermediate extension.
\item
  So if \(f\) is an irreducible factor of \(\phi_n\), \(f\) is the
  minimal polynomial of some root \(\alpha\) of \(\phi_n\), so
  \(\deg f \divides n\).
\item
  \(\phi_n'(x) = p^nx^{p^{n-1}} \neq 0\), so \(\phi_n\) is squarefree
  and thus has no repeated factors. So \(\phi_n\) is the product of all
  such irreducible \(f\).
\end{itemize}

\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Finite fields are not algebraically closed]

If \({\mathbb{F}}\) is a finite field then
\(F\neq \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muF\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}

If \(k = \left\{{ a_1, a_2, \cdots a_n }\right\}\) then define the
polynomial
\begin{align*}
f(x) \coloneqq 1 +\prod_{j=1}^n (x-a_j) \in k[x]
.\end{align*}
This has no roots in \(k\).

\end{proof}

\todo{Proof}

\hypertarget{cyclotomic-polynomials}{%
\subsection{Cyclotomic Polynomials}\label{cyclotomic-polynomials}}

\begin{definition}[Euler's Totient Function]

\begin{align*}
\phi(n) \coloneqq\# \left\{{ k\leq n {~\mathrel{\Big|}~}\gcd(k ,n ) = 1}\right\}
.\end{align*}

\end{definition}

\begin{remark}

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\phi(p) = p-1\), because every number \(k\leq p-1\) is coprime to
  \(p\).
\item
  \(\phi(p^k) = p^{k} - p^{k-1}\), since there are \(p^k\) total numbers
  less than \(p^k\), most of which are coprime to \(p\). The ones to
  remove are those dividing \(p^k\): the only divisors of \(p^k\) are
  \(p^\ell\) for \(0\leq \ell \leq k\), and \(\gcd(p^k, m) = p^\ell\)
  whenever \(m=tp\) for \(t = 1,2,3,\cdots,p^{k-1}\) (i.e.~\(m\) is
  divisible by some power of \(p\), so the \(p^{k-1}\) multiples of
  \(p\) are possible).
\item
  \(\phi\) is multiplicative (arithmetically, so only on prime powers!)
\end{itemize}

\end{remark}

\begin{example}[Some totient values]

\begin{align*}
\phi(1) &= 1 \\
\phi(2) &= 1 \\
\phi(3) &= 2 \\
\phi(4) &= 2 \\
\phi(6) &= 2 \\
\phi(8) &= 4 \\
.\end{align*}

\end{example}

\begin{definition}[Cyclotomic Polynomials]

Let \(\zeta_n = e^{2\pi i/n}\), then the \textbf{\(n\)th cyclotomic
polynomial} is given by
\begin{align*}
\Phi_{n}(x)=\prod_{k=1 \atop (j, n)=1}^{n}\left(x- \zeta_n^k\right) \in {\mathbb{Z}}[x]
,\end{align*}

which is a product over primitive roots of unity. It is the unique
irreducible polynomial which is a divisor of \(x^n - 1\) but \emph{not}
a divisor of \(x^k-1\) for any \(k<n\).

Note that \(\deg \Phi_n(x) = \phi(n)\) for \(\phi\) the totient
function.

\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Cyclotomic Field]

Any subfield of \(\operatorname{SF}(x^n-1)\) is a \textbf{cyclotomic
field}.

\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Computing Cyclotomic Polynomials]

\textbf{Computing \(\Phi_n\):}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item

  \begin{align*}
  \Phi_{n}(z)=\prod_{\substack{ d \divides n \\  d > 0} }\left(z^{d}-1\right)^{\mu\left(\frac{n}{d}\right)}
  \end{align*}
  where
  \begin{align*}
  \mu(n) \equiv\left\{ \begin{array}{ll}{0} & {\text { if } n \text { has one or more repeated prime factors }} \\ {1} & {\text { if } n=1} \\ {(-1)^{k}} & {\text { if } n \text { is a product of } k \text { distinct primes, }}\end{array}\right.
  \end{align*}
\item

  \begin{align*}
  x^{n}-1=\prod_{d | n} \Phi_{d}(x) \implies \Phi_n(x) = \qty{x^n-1} \qty{\prod_{d | n \atop d < n} \Phi_{d}(x)}^{-1},
  \end{align*}
  so just use polynomial long division.
\end{enumerate}

\end{proposition}

\begin{fact}[computing cyclotomic polynomials, special cases and examples]

\begin{align*}
\Phi_{p}(x)   &=  x^{p-1}+x^{p-2}+\cdots+x+1 \\
\Phi_{2 p}(x) &=  x^{p-1}-x^{p-2}+\cdots-x+1 \\
\\
k\divides n \implies \Phi_{n}(x) &= \Phi_{n\over k}\left(x^{k}\right)
\\ \\
\Phi_1(z) &= z-1 \\
\Phi_2(z) &= z+1 \\
\Phi_4(z) &= z^2+1 \\
\Phi_6(z) &= z^2 -z + 1 \\
\Phi_8(z) &= z^4+1 
.\end{align*}

\end{fact}

\begin{proposition}[Splitting Fields of Cyclotomic Polynomials]

The splitting field of \(x^m-1\) is \({\mathbb{Q}}(\zeta_m)\) for
\(\zeta_m\) any primitive root of unity, and
\begin{align*}
{ \mathsf{Gal}} ({\mathbb{Q}}(\zeta_m)_{/{\mathbb{Q}}}) \cong ({\mathbb{Z}}/m{\mathbb{Z}})^{\times}
.\end{align*}

\end{proposition}

\begin{theorem}[Kronecker-Weber]

If \(K_{/{\mathbb{Q}}}\) is an abelian extension, then
\(K \subseteq {\mathbb{Q}}(\zeta_m)\) for some \(m\).

\end{theorem}

\hypertarget{misc}{%
\subsection{Misc}\label{misc}}

\begin{proposition}[?]

If \(f\in k[x]^{{\mathrm{irr}}}\) with \(\operatorname{ch}k = p\), then
there is a unique separable \(g\in k[x]^{{\mathrm{irr}}}\) such that
\(f(x) = g(x^{p^k})\) for some unique \(k\).

\end{proposition}

\begin{definition}[Elementary Symmetric Functions]

\todo[inline]{todo}

\end{definition}

\hypertarget{exercises-2}{%
\subsection{Exercises}\label{exercises-2}}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show
\begin{align*}
x^\ell - 1 \divides x^m-1 \iff \ell\divides m
.\end{align*}

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

\(\implies\) - Write \(m = \ell q + r\) with \(0\leq r < \ell\). - Write
\begin{align*}
p(x) = {x^m-1 \over x^\ell - 1}
= {x^{lq+r} -1 \over x^\ell - 1}
= x^r{x^{lq} - 1 \over x^\ell - 1} + {x^r - 1 \over x^\ell - 1}
= q(x) + {x^r-1 \over x^\ell - 1}
,\end{align*}
where \(p,q\) are polynomial by divisibility. - So the remaining ratio
must be polynomial, but since \(r<\ell\) is strict this forces \(r=0\).
Thus \(\ell \divides m\).

\(\impliedby\):

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Write \(m = \ell q + r\), then \(r=0\) by divisibility.
\item
  Then \(x^m-1 = x^{\ell q} - 1 \coloneqq z^q-1\) where
  \(z\coloneqq x^\ell\).
\item
  Use that \(z-1 \divides z^q - 1\), so
  \(x^{\ell}-1 \divides x{\lq} -1 = x^m-1\).
\end{itemize}

\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}[?]

Show that if \(f \in {\mathbb{F}}_p[x]^{{\mathrm{irr}}}\) is degree
\(d\),
\begin{align*}
f \divides x^{p^n}-x \iff d\divides n
.\end{align*}

\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}

\(\impliedby\):

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(d\divides n\), \(x^d-1 \divides x^n-1\) by a previous exercise,
  and so \(p^d-1 \divides p^n-1\).
\item
  So \(x^{p^d-1} \divides x^{p^n-1}\), now multiply through by \(x\).
\item
  Claim: \(f \divides x^{p^d-1}\), from which the result immediately
  follows.
\item
  For \(\alpha\) any root of \(f\), \({\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha)\) is a
  finite field of size \(p^d\) since
  \([{\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha):{\mathbb{F}}_p] = d\).
\item
  So \({\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha)\cong {\mathbb{GF}}(p^d)\), which is the
  splitting field of \(x^{p^d}-x\).
\item
  Thus \(\alpha\) is a root of \(x^{p^d}-x\). Iterating over all roots
  yields the divisibility statement.
\end{itemize}

\(\implies\):

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(f\divides g_n(x) \coloneqq x^{p^n}-x\), then every root
  \(\alpha\) of \(f\) is a root of \(g_n\).
\item
  So \({\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha) \subseteq {\mathbb{GF}}(p^n)\).
\item
  The result follows from the computation
  \begin{align*}
  n &= [{\mathbb{GF}}(p^n) : {\mathbb{F}}_p] \\
  &= [{\mathbb{GF}}(p^n) : {\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha)] \cdot [{\mathbb{F}}_p(\alpha) : {\mathbb{F}}_p] \\
  &= kd
  .\end{align*}
\end{itemize}

\end{solution}

\hypertarget{field-theory-extensions-and-towers}{%
\section{Field Theory: Extensions and
Towers}\label{field-theory-extensions-and-towers}}

\hypertarget{basics-1}{%
\subsection{Basics}\label{basics-1}}

\begin{remark}

Galois is defined as \textbf{normal and separable}.

\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Simple Extensions]

An extension \(L/k\) is \textbf{simple} iff \(L = K( \alpha)\) for some
\(\alpha\in L\).

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Primitive Element Theorem]

Every finite separable extension is simple.

\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}

\({\mathbb{GF}}(p^n)\) is a simple extension over \({\mathbb{F}}_p\).

\end{corollary}

\begin{definition}[Algebraic Extension]

A field extension \(L/k\) is \textbf{algebraic} iff every
\(\alpha \in L\) is the root of some polynomial \(f\in k[x]\).

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Finite Extensions are Algebraic]

Every finite extension is algebraic.

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[that finite extensions are algebraic]

If \(K/F\) and \([K:F] = n\), then pick any \(\alpha \in K\) and
consider \(1, \alpha , \alpha ^2, ...\). This yields \(n+1\) elements in
an \(n{\hbox{-}}\)dimensional vector space, and thus there is a linear
dependence
\begin{align*}
f(\alpha ) \coloneqq\sum_{j=1}^n c_j \alpha ^j = 0
.\end{align*}
But then \(\alpha\) is the root of the polynomial \(f\).

\end{proof}

\hypertarget{normal-extensions}{%
\subsection{Normal Extensions}\label{normal-extensions}}

\begin{definition}[Normal Field Extension]

Let \(L/k\) be an extension. Then TFAE:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(L/k\) is \textbf{normal}.
\item
  Every irreducible polynomial \(f\in k[x]\) that has one root in \(L\)
  has \emph{all} of its roots in \(L\), and thus splits in \(L[x]\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    So if \(\alpha\in L\) then every Galois conjugate \(\alpha_k \in L\)
    as well.. Thus either \(f\) splits in \(L\) or \(f\) has no roots in
    \(L\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[of normal extensions]

\envlist

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Useful trick: if \([L: k] = 2\) then \(L/k\) is automatically normal.
\item
  Useful trick: if \(L/K/k\), then \(K/k\) is normal iff
  \({ \mathsf{Gal}} (L/K) {~\trianglelefteq~}{ \mathsf{Gal}} (L/k)\).
\item
  \(K \coloneqq{\mathbb{Q}}(2^{1\over 3})\) is not normal, since
  \(K\subset {\mathbb{R}}\) but
  \((x^3-2) = \prod_k x-\zeta_3^k 2^{1\over 3}\) with
  \(\zeta_3, \zeta_3^2 \in {\mathbb{C}}\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Another reason: an embedding
    \(\sigma: K\to \mkern 1.5mu\overline{\mkern-1.5muk\mkern-1.5mu}\mkern 1.5mu\)
    can send \(2^{1\over 3}\) to any other root of \(x^3-2\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \({\mathbb{Q}}(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)\) is normal over \({\mathbb{Q}}\),
  since it it is finite and splits \(f(x) \coloneqq(x^2-2)(x^2-3)\),
  which is a separable polynomial.
\item
  \(L \coloneqq{\mathbb{Q}}(2^{1\over 4})\) is not normal, since it is
  finite but not the splitting field of any polynomial.
\item
  \({\mathbb{Q}}(\zeta_k)\) is normal for \(\zeta_k\) any primitive
  \(k\)th root of unity.
\item
  A normal non-separable extension:
  \({\mathbb{F}}_p(x, y) _{/ {{\mathbb{F}}_p (x^p, y^p)}}\). This has
  finite degree \(p^2\) but infinitely many subfields?
\end{itemize}

\end{example}
